Plant and Joint Reuse Facilities," and " – Iron Bridge Plant" below. 
Biosolids Disposal.  Biosolids are a by-product of the wastewater treatment process and 
must be disposed of in a manne r which complies with FDEP a nd U.S. Environmental Protection 
Agency ("EPA") regulations.  Beneficial use of  biosolids is accomplished by encouraging land 
application of treated biosolids for agricultura l purposes.  The City's biosolids program has 
consistently met the requirements of FDEP and EPA and has provided a valuable organic fertilizer 
supplement to the local agricultu ral community.  The City contra cts with private haulers to 
transport, further treat and landfill or land apply the City's biosolids at FDEP approved sites.  This 
arrangement allows the City access to a wide ra nge of land application options that otherwise 
might not be available. 
Reclaimed Water System 
Reclaimed Water.  Reclaimed water is domestic wa stewater which has been treated, 
filtered, and disinfected to  a high degree such that it can be safe ly used to irrigate golf courses, 
parks, residential lawns, and other public access areas.  The City produces reclaimed water at each 
of the WRFs, and then pumps the reclaimed water through a network of pipes to end-users.  The 
Conserv I Plant and the Conserv II  Plant direct 100% of their effluent into reuse, while the Iron 
Bridge Plant currently directs 70% of its effluent into reuse. 
Customers.  Customers of the Recl aimed Water System include  single-family homes, 
multi-family homes, commercial properties, indus trial facilities, golf courses, theme parks, 
schools, and hotels.  There are approximately 7,407 resident ial and commercial accounts 
connected to the Reclaimed Water System.  The majority of the usage is for golf courses and 
single-family residential neighbor hoods located in Baldwin Park and the Lake Nona area of 
southeast Orlando.  The Orlando Intern ational Airport is also a larg e user of reclaimed water for 
irrigating landscaping.  
35 
Transmission and Distribution System .  The reclaimed water distribution systems are 
separated into the eastern and western areas.  Th e Iron Bridge Plant and the Conserv I Plant feed 
the City-owned Eastern Regional Reclaimed Water Distribution System ("ERRWDS"), which was 
commissioned in 2011 and provides reclaimed water, in conjunction with Orange County's Eastern 
WRF and the Conserv I Plant, to portions of Seminole and Orange Counties, the Greater Orlando 
Aviation Authority ("GOAA"), and neighborhoods lo cated in Baldwin Park and the Lake Nona 
area of southeast Orlando.  The ERRWDS is a regi onal system that delivers water to the City's 
reclaimed water customers as we ll as multiple other reclaimed water utility providers.  For 
additional information relating to the ERRWDS, see "THE WATER RECLAMATION 
SYSTEM – Conserv I Plant - Growth Potential and Limitations" below.  The western reclaimed 
water distribution system is fed solely from the Conserv II Plant and delivers water to local reuse 
and the Conserv II Plant distribution center located in Winter Garden, Florida.  Combined, the two 
reclaimed water distribution syst ems comprise approximately 215 mi les of reclaimed water pipe 
ranging in size from 1-inch to 48-inches in diameter.  The aforementioned interconnect systems in 
the Wastewater System allow the City to transf er wastewater flow between the three plants, 
essentially allowing the City to shift the quantity of reclaimed water available to the east and west 
portions of the Reclaimed Water System. 
Historical Flows.  The Reclaimed Water System has continued to grow since its inception.  
The City enacted an ordinance in 2002 that requires reclaimed water piping to be installed in new 
residential and commercial developments if the development falls within the Reclaimed Water 
System Service Area (the "2002 Ordinance").  The same ordinance requires customer connection 
to the Reclaimed Water System for irrigation wh en reclaimed water is made available to the 
property.  The impact of these efforts will increase reclaimed water use and reduce the amount of 
potable water used for irrigati on.  The following table presents reclaimed water flows for the 
Conserv I Plant, the Conserv II Plant, and the Iron Bridge Plant for cale ndar years 2019 through 
2023.

Conserv I Plant 
General.  The Conserv I Plant serves residentia l and commercial developments along the 
S.R. 436 corridor and around the Orlando Interna tional Airport in southeast Orlando.  The  
36 
Conserv I Plant includes wastewater trans mission facilities, a 7.5 MGD advanced water 
reclamation facility, and an effl uent disposal system utilizing head-induced lateral percolation 
basins and reclaimed water irrigation.  The current treatment capacity of the Conserv I Plant is 
expected to be sufficient to meet wastewater demands in the Wastewater System Service Area of 
the Conserv I Plant until 2030.  Based on projected growth in the Wastewater System Service Area 
of the Conserv I Plant beyond this time horizon, the City is planning to expand the Conserv I Plant 
to a capacity of 15 MGD within the next five years. 
The Conserv I Plant utilizes the following processes: 
 Pretreatment by screening and grit removal; 
 Flow equalization; 
 Activated sludge; 
 Secondary clarification; 
 Dual media filtration; 
 Chlorination for disinfection; 
 Sludge thickening and transport to Conserv II Plant/Iron Bridge Plant for stabilization, 
dewatering, and disposal; and 
 Standby power generation. 
During the first ten years of operation of the Conserv I Plant, the primary means of effluent 
reuse was groundwater recharge through a system of 15 percolation basins located on the Orlando 
International Airport property near the treatment plant.  In 1995, all of the basins were improved 
through the addition of a liner an d the placement of gravel on th e interior side slopes (the 
"1995 Improvements").  These modifications have successfully extended the operating capacity of 
these basins. 
The City has since constructed the ERRWDS which now serves as the primary means of 
effluent reuse for the Conserv I Plant.  For ad ditional information relating to the ERRWDS, see 
"THE WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEM  – Reclaimed Water System – Transmission and 
Distribution System" above.  The percolation basins are used primarily for substandard reclaimed 
water disposal or for alternat ive disposal during severe or prolonged rainfall periods when 
irrigation is significantly reduced.  The 1995 Im provements enhance the capability of the 
percolation basins to serve in such capacity. 
In 2021, the City initiated the design process for the rehabilitation and expansion of the 
Conserv I Plant with the selection of Wharton Smith and AECOM as the design-builder through a 
competitive proposal process.  The initial design effort was based on upgrading the Conserv I Plant 
to a 10 MGD facility.  Since that time, there ha s been significant devel opment in the Conserv I 
Plant service area within the Wastewater System Service Area and current projections support the 
need to increase the plant capacity to 15 MGD.  Th e City has instructed the design-build team to 
modify their efforts to reflect such increase in plant size.  The City anticipates that the design-build 
team will initiate construction in late 2025 an d complete the Conserv I Plant expansion by 
mid-2029.  The costs of such construction are still being evaluated and City staff will be developing 
a funding strategy for these impro vements once revenue growth tied to development in the 
Conserv I Plant service area within the Wastewa ter System Service Area and project costs are  
37 
better defined.  See "THE WATER RECL AMATION SYSTEM – Conserv I Plant – Growth 
Potential and Limitations" below.

The City received its initial FDEP operating pe rmit for the Conserv I Plant and effluent 
disposal system on October l, 1991 for 7.5 MGD.  Operating permits  are issued by the FDEP for 
a period of five years.  The C ity has timely applied for and received all operating permits since 
1991.  The most recent permit was issued on March 2, 2021, with an  effective date of April 12, 
2021.  The permit expires on April 11, 2026 and the City anticipates timely applying for and 
receiving a new permit.  The Conserv I Plant curre ntly meets or exceeds the requirements of its 
operating permit regarding effluent quality.  It is the opinion of the City and Consulting Engineer 
that the Conserv I Plant is capable of continuing to meet the existing FDEP operating permit 
numerical standards.  
39 
Growth Potential and Limitations .  The City entered into an Amended and Restated 
Operation and Use Agreement dated August 31, 2015 (the "GOAA Operation and Use 
Agreement"), pursuant to which the City has granted to GOAA certain rights and responsibilities 
to operate and control certain City-owned land for GOAA's operation of the Orlando Executive 
Airport and Orlando International Airport, which land includes the approximately 186 acre site of 
the Conserv I Plant located within the southwest corner of the Orlando International Airport, and 
pursuant to which the City has reserved the right to construct and operate the Conserv I Plant, and 
all related percolation basin sites.  The GOAA Op eration and Use Agreement allows the City to 
utilize the percolation basin sites.  The GOA A Operation and Use Agreement also gives GOAA 
the right to take back any percolation basin site for airport purposes and request 
demolition/removal of the percolation basins at any time during the term of the GOAA Operation 
and Use Agreement, in which case GOAA is required to assist the City in locating an equivalent 
alternate basin site.  Due to airport expansion needs, GOAA has reclaimed many of the percolation 
basin sites.  With the construction of the ERRWDS , the need for the percolation basins has been 
reduced to providing storage during inclement weather events or disposal of effluent that does not 
meet irrigation standards. 
The demand for wastewater treatment capacity in the Wastewater System Service Area of 
the Conserv I Plant is expected to double by 2050.  Under the requirements of the 2002 Ordinance, 
new development will be required to install reclaimed water irrigation systems and the ERRWDS 
will be expanded to those areas.  Reclaimed wa ter irrigation through the ERRWDS will continue 
to serve as the primary means of effluent reuse for the Conserv I Plant.  Expansion of the Conserv I 
Plant from 7.5 MGD to 15 MGD will, however, require  the City to investigate additional options 
to supplement the percol ation basins for inclement weather disposal.  The City has a hired a 
consulting engineer to review available options.  Such options could include transferring reclaimed 
water through the ERRWDS to connect with the transmission main from the Iron Bridge Plant to 
the wetlands treatment system or seeking a wet weather discharge permit into a local water body 
that can accommodate the flow.  The City and consulting engineer have solicited input from the 
FDEP and anticipate receiving a response by late 2024. 
Conserv II Plant and Joint Reuse Project 
General.  The Conserv II facilities include the Conserv II Plant, a 25 MGD facility, and a 
50 MGD water reuse system project, which is a joint project equally shared between the City and 
Orange County (the "Joint Reuse Project"). 
The Conserv II Plant serves residential and commercial development generally west of 
Interstate 4 and is located on McLeod Road between Kirkman Road and Bruton Boulevard.  An 
interlocal agreement between the City and Orange County entered into on July 28, 1983, provided 
for the design, construction, and op eration of the Joint Reuse Proj ect (the "Joint Reuse Project 
Agreement").  Pursuant to the Joint Reuse Project Agreement, the "joint facilities" are defined to 
include effluent transmission pumping stations, approximately 21.5 miles of transmission pipeline, 
a distribution center, the distribution network, and a series of rapid infiltration basins ("RIBs"). 
In order to meet the "no discharge" requirement of the FDEP, the Conserv II Plant was 
designed to produce advanced secondary effluent that is suitable for public access irrigation and 
groundwater recharge.  As originally conceived, the Conserv II Plant was designed to ultimately  
40 
deliver up to 25 MGD of reclaimed water for irrigation of 12,000 to 15,000 acres of citrus groves 
and groundwater recharge through RIBs.  The City expects the Conserv II Plant to have sufficient 
capacity to meet wastewater needs within the Wastewater System Service Area of the Conserv II 
Plant through the year 2035.  Currently, the Conserv II Plant utilizes the following treatment 
processes: 
 Pretreatment by screening and grit removal; 
 Flow equalization; 
 Activated sludge with nitrification and denitrification; 
 Secondary clarification; 
 Effluent flow equalization; 
 Automatic backwash dual media filtration; 
 High level chlorination for disinfection; 
 Sludge thickening and dewatering with stabilization and hauling provided via 
contracted services; and 
 Standby power generation. 
The Conserv II Plant has received numerous upgrades since its completion in 1986.  Recent 
projects include the big bubble aeration mixing project which was completed in 2016.  Such project 
allows the treatment plant to mix anoxic treatme nt zones with large bubble cannons instead of 
using fine bubble diffusers, further enhancing the Conserv II Plant's nutrient reduction capabilities.  
Another major construction project, which was completed in 2020, involved biosolids processing 
improvements.  Such project involved replacing the existing gravity belt thickeners and belt filter 
presses with three advanced belt filter presses as well  as replacing the existing return activated 
sludge and waste activated sludge pumping stations. 
Construction continues on the new Pretreatme nt Structure with new screens and grit 
equipment at the Conserv II Plant.  The structure is nearing completion.  The motor control centers 
have been installed, control panel installation and programming are underway, grit snails, screw 
compactors, and grit pumps have also been inst alled, yard piping is co mplete, and a tie-in and 
bypass plan is currently being developed to ensure plant operations remain online while the system 
is started up and tested.  The project is expected to be completed in January of 2025.

The most recent operating permit for the Conserv II Plant was issued on December 5, 2021 
and will expire on December 5,  2026.  The City anticipates tim ely applying for and receiving a 
renewal of the operating permit. 
With respect to the Conserv II Plant future water quality permit requirements, the Joint 
Reuse Project RIBs fall within the Wekiwa and Rock Springs basin management action plan 
("BMAP") area.  The BMAP requires a reduction in  nutrient concentrations  for effluent being 
discharged within the basin that could impact the springs area.  The current permit reflects this 
requirement with effluent quality limits of 5 mg/l CBOD, 5 mg/l TSS, 3 mg/l total nitrogen, and 
1 mg/l total phosphorus.  Although the Conserv II Plant is currently capable of meeting these limits 
with the existing influent flow, the process will need to be modified once the plant approaches a  
43 
flow level of 20 MGD.  The City has hired a consulting engineer to perform an alternatives analysis 
on process modifications necessary to achieve the required permit limits at the full plant flow of 
25 MGD.  The City anticipates receiving recommendations in mid-2025 and will program design 
and construction of the necessary improvements within the next ten years. 
City management and design consulting engineers have concluded that the Conserv II Plant 
currently meets or exceeds the requirements of its operating permit regarding effluent quality and 
that the Conserv II Plant is capable of continuing to meet these effluent requirements through 2035 
with the modifications described above.

agreement with the City of Winter Park to pr ovide wastewater treatment capacity within the 
Conserv II Plant for portions of the City of Winter Park that are impractical for them to serve and 
are in close proximity to the Wastewater System  Service Area of the Conserv II Plant.  The

Water Reuse Customers.  The Joint Reuse Project is beneficial to both the City and Orange 
County (each of which have half of the 50 MGD capacity in the Joint Reuse Project).  The original 
Joint Reuse Project involved irrigation of citrus and other agricultural crops in west Orange County 
and east Lake County, Florida ("Lake County").  As a result of significant freeze events and a 
series of citrus diseases, the citrus industry has been significantly reduced in the Joint Reuse Project 
area and is no longer a major user of reclaimed water.  In many cases, property that was formerly 
planted in citrus has been sold for developmen t and the Joint Reuse Proj ect irrigation customer 
base has transitioned to residential communities.  This transition has resulted in no net loss of 
irrigation capacity under the Joint Reuse Project permit. 
The Joint Reuse Project is currently permitt ed for 39.0 MGD (average annual daily flow) 
of disposal capacity through the public access reuse system. 
Properties served by the Joint Reuse Project in Lake County require a conditional use 
permit ("CUP") under Lake County's Zoning Code.  Participating customers must obtain a CUP 
for those parcels that are included in the agreemen ts.  All of the particip ating customers in Lake 
County have received their CUPs.  The City and Or ange County have agreed to comply with the 
conditions set forth in the CUPs, including th e implementation of a groundwater-monitoring 
program.  Groundwater monitoring wells have been installed at alternate application sites that have  
44 
been approved by FDEP and Lake County.  Samples are collected quarterly and routinely analyzed 
for specific parameters. 
In 2000, the City and Orange County purchased 2,400 acres of land from Hi-Acres in Lake 
County.  These parcels, which were under the prev ious grower's agreement for irrigation, will 
provide further flexibility for the wet weather dis posal capacity for the Joint Reuse Project.  The 
land will be used in the future for some combination of RIB systems and agricultural irrigation.  
The first series of RIBs to be constructed on th e property were completed in late 2004.  The City 
and Orange County jointly own a total of 5,400 acres of land for current and future RIB sites.  The 
Joint Reuse Project is currently permitted for a RIB capacity of 29.2 MGD (average annual daily 
flow). 
Local Area City Contracts .  The City has entered into reclaimed water agreements with 
other major users in clos e proximity to the Conser v II Plant that are not part of the Joint Reuse 
Project joint facilities.  One of the users, the MetroWest Master Community Association, accepts 
and uses up to 1.5 MGD of reclaimed water on its  golf course and median green spaces.  In 
addition, the City supplies reclaimed water to Valencia Community College, Universal Orlando 
Resorts, and other users in the area.  Total annu al reclaimed water usage for these customers is 
approximately 4.65 MGD and is expected to incr ease as the Water Reclamation System is 
expanded. 
Growth Potential and Limitations .  The ability to expand the Conserv II Plant treatment 
capacity is directly related to the ability to find additional customers to connect to the distribution 
network or to the construction of additional RIBs.  The City foresees no difficulties in achieving 
this as demand for irrigation in the Joint Reuse Project area is significant. 
The City and Orange County have plans fo r expanding RIB capacity on project land in 
Lake County.  The City and Orange County w ill be working with Lake County on gaining 
approvals for the construction of additional RIBs in the future.  Construction of additional RIB 
sites will add wet-weather capacity to the Water Reclamation System, further ensuring the City's 
ability to provide service during extreme weather conditions.  Another option being considered for 
future management of recla imed water during reduced demand periods is long term storage 
through the creation of a reservoir on project land currently owned by the City and Orange County. 
Iron Bridge Plant 
The Iron Bridge Plant is a regional water recl amation facility, which serves the east and 
central portions of the City, unincorporated areas of Orange and Seminole Counties, and portions 
of the Cities of Casselberry, Maitland, and Winter Park.  The City expects the Iron Bridge Plant to 
have sufficient capacity to meet wastewater ne eds in the Wastewater System Service Area of the 
Iron Bridge Plant through the year 2035. 
The Iron Bridge Plant is a 40 MGD advanced water reclamation facility, which employs 
three different effluent disposal means (waste lo ad allocation limit on the discharge to the Little 
Econlockhatchee River and performance standards for the artificial wetlands facility which flows 
ultimately to the St. Johns River and the ERRWDS).  
45 
The Iron Bridge Plant is located off Alafaya Tr ail northwest of the University of Central 
Florida campus in south Seminole County.  The plant utilizes the following processes: 
 Pretreatment by screening and grit removal; 
 Flow equalization; 
 Fermentation; 
 1st anoxic zone; 
 Aeration zone; 
 2nd anoxic zone; 
 2nd aerobic zone; 
 Secondary clarification; 
 Deep Bed filtration; 
 Chlorination for disinfection/Dechlorination for outfall; 
 Post aeration; 
 Sludge dewatering with stabilization and hauling provided via contracted services; 
and 
 Standby power generation. 
The Iron Bridge Plant has received numerous upgrades to improve process performance 
and replace aging and obsolete equipment.  Two construction projects ar e planned for the near 
future.  The first project will be the replacem ent of the existing grit removal system though a 
design-build contract (t he "Iron Bridge Grit Replacement Project").  The Iron Bridge Grit 
Replacement Project involves the construction of a new grit removal facility that will incorporate 
the latest grit removal technology capable of removing finer grit particles than the existing system.  
The Iron Bridge Grit Replacement Project is in permitting review and the City anticipates issuing 
the notice to proceed for construction in Sept ember 2024.  The Iron Bridge Grit Replacement 
Project is one of the projects anticipated to be funded with a portion of the proceeds of the Series 
2024A Bonds. 
The second project involves the replacement of mechanisms and sludge collection systems 
in eight secondary clarifiers. The replacement of the mechanisms will be performed under a 
design-build contract.  The design-build firm has been selected and the City is currently negotiating 
the design services contract which is anticipated to receive City Council approval by October 2024.  
The project will improve the capacity and solids captu re characteristics of the clarifiers and is 
anticipated to take 10 months to construct with a tentative completion date of September 2026. 
Two different effluent outfalls and the ERRWDS are available to the Iron Bridge Plant for 
effluent management.  The original facility had an NPDES permitted discharge of 24 MGD which 
provided for both concentration and total pounds per day restrictions on the effluent discharged to 
the Little Econlockhatchee River.  To allow for expansion of the Iron Bridge Plant, the City 
designed and constructed a 20 MGD wetlands treatment facility (the "Wetlands Facility") on a 
1,650-acre site near Christmas, Florida.  A 17-mile, 48-inch force main was constructed to transmit 
the treated effluent from the Iron Bridge Plant to the  head of the cell-oriented wetlands system.  
Totaling 1,220 acres, the Wetlands Facility was developed with a series of cells divided by earthen 
berms and planted with different wetland ve getation to create thr ee separate wetlands 
communities, which provide nitrogen and phosphorous removal.  The Wetlands Facility has been  
46 
operating well within the anticipated performan ce guidelines since beco ming operational in 
September 1987.  An indication of the positive performance of the We tlands Facility is the fact 
that FDEP increased the permitte d capacity of the Wetlands Facil ity from the initial level of 
8 MGD to 20 MGD during the 1990's and in 2001 re-r ated the capacity of th e Wetlands Facility 
from 20 MGD to 35 MGD.  The majority of the Iron Bridge Plant flow is directed to either the 
ERRWDS or the Wetlands Facility.  The discharge to the Little Econlockhatchee River is reserved 
primarily for wet weather events in compliance with current FDEP nutrient reduction regulations.

Major Wholesale Customers and Interlocal Agreements .  The City and its five 
governmental entity partners (Orange and Semi nole Counties and the C ities of Casselberry, 
Maitland and Winter Park) have entered into various interlocal agreements related to the 
acceptance, treatment, and disposal of wastewater at the Iron Bridge Plant.  The City has also 
entered into an interlocal agreement with the South Seminole & North Orange County Wastewater 
Transmission Authority, which accepts wastewater from its participants and transmits it to the Iron 
Bridge Plant.  The agreements are essentially uniform in nature as to the procedure for allocation 
of capacity at the plant and payment for said capacity.  The agreements also set out the amount of 
capacity for each party and provide a formula for temporary and permanent reallocation.  The

Each entity is committed to pay for its share of the capital costs based on allocated capacity 
regardless of actual flows.  In addition, each entity pays an operation and maintenance cost based 
on actual flows.  Relief available to the City  for non-payment by any entity is provided by a 
$500,000 escrow account funded pro rata by the entities.  The agreements allow for the various  
51 
entities to expand the plant capacity  for their needs at their expens e regardless of whether or not 
the City requires expanded capacity. 
Growth Potential and Limitations.  The current Iron Bridge Plant capacity rating appears 
to be sufficient to meet the future needs of the City and its partners through 2035.  The City's focus 
will be on maintaining and updating the existing plant infrastructure and adding facilities, such as 
expanded flow equalization, to improve the reliability and performance of the plant as a whole. 
Should expansion be needed in the future, the City believes that the 40 MGD facility may 
be re-rated at nominal costs to further enable the City and its participating entities to properly 
address their growth-related needs. 
Recent Extreme Weather Event Affecting the Water Reclamation System 
Hurricane Milton made landfall on October 9, 2024 in Siesta Key near Sarasota, Florida, 
which is approximately 140 road miles southwest of Orland o, as a Category 3 Hurricane.  The 
center of Hurricane Milton passed south of Orlando as a Category 1 storm with hurricane-force 
wind gusts and rainfall impacting the Orlando municipal area.  The City has a proactive emergency 
management plan and response to extreme weather events such as hurricanes.  Additionally, the 
City made significant capital investments in the Water Reclamation System in recent years making 
the Water Reclamation System more resilient to extreme weather events.  Based on the 
assessments that the City has completed to date , most of the City was not significantly impacted 
by flooding and damage from rain and wind is not expected to be significant.  
In regard to overflows, the impacts of Hurri cane Milton caused one significant overflow 
which was attributed to stormwater being inadvert ently introduced into the sanitary system.  The 
volume of this overflow is still being estimated but is likely to be several hundred thousand gallons.  
During the storm, the Water Reclamation Syst em pumped approximately 147 MGD of untreated 
wastewater, inflow and infiltration.  The aforementioned overflow represents less than 0.3 percent 
of flow carried by the Water Reclamation System as a result of Hurricane  Milton.  The City has 
already reported such unauthorized spills to FDEP as has been its historical practice.  Based on the 
City's past experience with FD EP, the unpermitted sanitary sewer overflows may result in 
regulatory action by FDEP in the future.  
While the City continues to assess the impact Hurricane Milton had on the Water 
Reclamation System and any expected storm-re lated costs relating to the Water Reclamation 
System, the City expects to have sufficient resources to meet its storm related financial obligations 
relating to the Water Reclamation System and its payment obligations relating to the Series 2024 
Bonds.  The City has experience with and will likely seek public assistance from the Federal 
Emergency Management Agency and the Florida Division of Emergency Management to recover 
some or all of its expenses.  For general info rmation relating to extreme weather events, see 
"CERTAIN INVESTMENTS CONSIDERATIONS – Extreme Weather Events" herein.

WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEM REVENUES 
Wastewater System Rate Structure 
The City allocates costs among all users by using a capacity/commodity method the City 
has utilized since 1984.  This method distinguishes between capacity (capital) costs and 
commodity (operating) costs.  Capital costs are identified and allocated to each user through a 
fixed monthly capacity charge ba sed on Equivalent Residential Connections ("ERC's").  For 
residential users (both single and multifamily), a dwelling unit is equal to one ERC. One ERC is 
equal to 7,000 gallons per month.  For commercial customers, the number of ERC's is determined 
based on a 12-month moving average of flows.  In addition, operating, maintenance and equipment 
replacement costs are recovered through a usage-based commodity ch arge.  Each user is billed 
based on each 1,000 gallons of actual water use.  Both commodity charges and capacity charges 
are included in Gross Revenues. 
Single-family residences are billed for wa stewater using only the first 14,000 gallons of 
metered water usage per month on the assumption that usage above 14,000 gallons is for 
non-wastewater purposes, such as lawn irrigation.  There are two separate  classes of multifamily 
billings.  Flat rate multifamily units are bill ed a monthly fee covering the capacity charge and 
commodity charges based upon an assumed usage of 4,200 gallons per month for one bedroom 
and efficiency apartment units and 6,000  gallons per month for two or more bedroom apartment 
units.  Master-metered multifamily units, like commercial users, are billed on the basis of metered 
water usage. 
Current City Code (as defined herein) ha s a provision whereby wastewater rates will 
automatically increase by 5% each year unless such increases are not deemed necessary by the 
City Council.  In Fiscal Year 2022, no increase was adopted based on the strong financial standing 
of the Water Reclamation System.  For Fisc al Years 2023 through 2025, the 5% increase was 
adopted. 
The City's rate structure includes a 25% surcharge for out-of-City customers as authorized 
under Florida Statutes, Section 180.191.  This rate differential is based on the higher cost of 
servicing out-of-City cu stomers and in consideration of the contribution in taxes paid by the 
In-City customers, particularly through the Utilities Serv ices Tax which is a surcharge levied on 
In-City utilities bills.  Approximately 28% of the flow into the Wastewater System comes from 
customers who reside outside of the City limits.   However, most of these are served through 
wholesale agreements with other governmental entities and are billed by those entities under their 
own rate structures.

Utilities Services Tax 
General.  State law authorizes any municipality in the State to levy a utilities service tax 
on the purchase within such municipality of elect ricity, metered natural ga s, liquefied petroleum 
gas either metered or bottled, ma nufactured gas either metered or bottled, water service and fuel 
oil as well as any services competitive with those specifically enumerated.  Such utilities services 
tax may not exceed 10% of the payments received by the sellers of such utilities services from 
purchasers (except in the case of fuel oil, for which the maximum tax is four cents per gallon).   
Pursuant to the Constitution and laws of the State and the Code of the City of Orlando (the 
"City Code"), the City levies and imposes the Utilities Services Tax, within the incorporated area 
of the City at the rate of 10% on sales of all util ity services for which it is allowed to tax with the 
restriction that the tax on fuel oil cannot exceed four cents per gallon.  Pursuant to the City Code: 
(a) purchases by the United States Government, th e State, and all counties,  school districts, and 
municipalities of the State, and by public bodies exempted by law or court order, are exempt from 
the levy of the Utilities Services Tax; provided, however, governmental bodies which sell or resell 
taxable service to non-exempt end users must collect and remit the Utilities Services Tax; (b) the  
57 
following purchases are exempt and excluded from th e levy of the Utilities Services Tax: (i) the 
purchase of fuel oil for use as an aircraft engine fuel or propellant or for use in internal combustion 
engines, (ii) the purchase of natural gas, manufactured gas, or fuel oil by a public or private utility, 
either for resale or for use as fuel in the genera tion of electricity, (iii) the purchase of metered or 
bottled gas (natural liquefied pe troleum gas or manufactured gas) or fuel oil for agricultural 
purposes (for purposes of this exemption, "agr icultural purposes" means bona fide farming, 
pasture, grove or forestry operations, including  horticulture, floriculture viticulture, dairy, 
livestock, poultry, bee and aquacu lture); (c) purchases by any rec ognized church in the City for 
use exclusively for church purposes are exempt from the levy of the Utilities Services Tax; and (d) 
the Utilities Services Tax shall not be applied to any "fuel adju stment charge," which means all 
increases in the cost of utility services to the ultimate consumer resulting from an increase in the 
cost of fuel to the ut ility subsequent to October 1, 1973.  Stat e law provides that a municipality 
may exempt from the utilities services tax the first 500 kilowatt hours of electricity purchased per 
month for residential use.  The City has not adopted such an exemption. 
The Utilities Services Tax must be collected by  the seller from purchasers at the time of 
sale and remitted to the City as prescribed by the City Code.  The Utilities Services Tax will appear 
on a periodic bill rendered to consumers for electricity, metered or bottled gas (liquefied petroleum 
gas or manufactured gas), fuel oil or water service.  The seller is liable for the Utilities Services 
Tax due and not remitted to the C ity; provided, however, that the seller shall not be liable for the 
payment of such tax upon uncollected charges until such charges have been duly paid by the 
purchaser.

CERTAIN INVESTMENT CONSIDERATIONS 
The information in this section describes ce rtain factors affecting the Water Reclamation 
System and other considerations which may impact the payment of or security for the Series 2024 
Bonds.  The following discussion is not meant to be  an exhaustive list of the factors affecting the 
Water Reclamation System and other considerations which may impact the payment of or security 
for the Series 2024 Bonds and does not necessarily re flect the relative importance of the various 
factors and considerations; and the City discla ims any responsibility to advise prospective 
investors of such risks either as they may exist at the date of dissemination of this Official Statement 
or as they may appear or change from time to time in the future.  In addition, there can be no 
assurance that other factors or considerations not  discussed herein will not become relevant or 
material in the future.  Investors are advised to consider the following factors along with all other 
information described in this Official Statemen t or incorporated by reference herein when 
evaluating the Series 2024 Bonds. 
Uncertainties of Projections, Forecasts, and Assumptions 
The Consulting Engineer's Report contains certain assumptions, forecasts, and projections.  
See "APPENDIX A – CONSULTING ENGINEER'S REPORT" attached hereto.  Projected 
compliance with certain of the c ovenants contained in the Master  Bond Ordinance is also based 
upon assumptions and projections.  Projections and assumptions are inherently  subject to 
significant uncertainties.  Inevitably, some assump tions will not be rea lized and unanticipated 
events and circumstances may occur and actual results are likely to differ, perhaps materially, from 
those projected.  Accordingly, the projections co ntained in the Consulting Engineer's Report are 
not necessarily indicative of future performance, and neither the City nor the Consulting Engineer 
assumes any responsibility for the accuracy of such projections.  See "FORWARD-LOOKING 
STATEMENTS" herein.  
Environmental and Regulatory Issues 
The City is subject to federal and State wast ewater treatment and disposal requirements 
which, among other things, control contaminants in  wastewater effluents and reclaimed water, 
limit discharges of pollutants into surface an d ground waters, regulate th e quality of effluent 
discharged from sewage treatment facilities, an d limit the nature of waste materials discharged 
into the collection facilities.  The City is also su bject to federal, State, and local waste disposal 
requirements, which control the means of disposing solids generated by sewage treatment plants.  
Environmental requirements in general are becoming more stringen t and further or new 
requirements may substantially increase the cost of wastewater service by requiring changes in the  
64 
design or operation of existing or new facilities.  Future changes could result in reduced capacity 
of the Water Reclamation System, additional operations or capital expenditures, or a reduction in 
Revenues.  Further, while the City undertakes to operate the Water Reclamation System in 
compliance with applicable regulatory requirements, there is no assurance that the Water 
Reclamation System facilities now or in the future will be maintained in compliance with current 
or future regulatory requirements.  Failure to co mply with those requirements could result in, 
among other things, enforcement action and/or the imposition of financial penalties. 
Permits 
There is no assurance that permits for operation of major components of the Water 
Reclamation System will be renewe d or can be renewed.  Further, there is no assurance that the 
requirements for renewal of the permits will remain  the same prior to the time that renewal is 
required; a change in requirements could require additional expenditures for improvements.  See 
"THE WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEM – Conserv I Plant – Performance Standards and 
Compliance," " – Conserv II Plant and Joint Reuse Project – Performance Standards and 
Compliance," " – Conserv II Plant and Joint Reuse Project – Water Reuse Customers," and " – Iron 
Bridge Plant – Performance Standards and Compliance" herein.  In addition, the City is unable to 
determine whether any additional permits it applies fo r in the future will be  granted.  See "THE 
WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEM – Conserv I Plant – Growth Potential and Limitations " 
herein.   
Costs and Schedule of the 2024 Project and Other Capital Projects 
The estimated costs of, and the projected schedule for, the projects included in the Capital 
Improvement Plan, including the 2024 Project, and other cap ital improvement projects 
(collectively, the "Capital Projec ts") are subject to a number of un certainties.  The ability of the 
City to complete the Capital Projects may be  adversely affected by various factors including, 
without limitation: design and engineering errors, changes to the scope of the Capital Projects, 
delays in contract awards, mate rial shortages or delivery dela ys, supply chain issues, labor 
shortages, unforeseen site conditions, adverse weather conditions, contra ctor defaults, labor 
disputes, inflation, litigation, delays in permitting or inability to obtain necessary permits, casualty 
and environmental issues, and additional security improvements and associated costs mandated by 
governmental authorities.  See "MANA GEMENT AND EMPLOYEES OF THE WATER 
RECLAMATION SYSTEM – Employ ees," "CERTAIN INVESTMENTS 
CONSIDERATION – Permits," " – Extreme We ather Events," and "LITIGATION MATTERS" 
herein.  No assurance can be given that the Capita l Projects will not cost more than is currently 
estimated.  Any schedule delays or cost increase s could result in the n eed to issue additional 
indebtedness.  The successful implementation of certain of the Capital Projects requires the 
issuance of additional indebtedness and the receipt of future revenues.  No assurances can be given 
that these sources of funding will be available in the assumed amounts or in the assumed schedule.  
See "OUTSTANDING WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEM OBLIGATIONS" and "CAPITAL 
IMPROVEMENT PLAN" herein and "APPENDIX A – CONSULTING ENGINEER'S REPORT" 
attached hereto.  
65 
Extreme Weather Events 
The State and Central Florida ar e naturally susceptible to th e effects of extreme weather 
events and natural disasters including floods, dr oughts, tornadoes, and hurricanes, which could 
result in negative economic impacts on the Water Reclamation System.  For information relating 
to Hurricane Milton, see "THE WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEM – Recent Extreme Weather 
Event Affecting the Water Reclamation System."  No assurance can be given as to whether future 
extreme weather events will occur that could materially impact the operation of the Water 
Reclamation System and the Pledged Revenues available for the payment of the Series 2024 
Bonds.  However, to mitigate against such imp acts, the City periodi cally undertakes risk 
assessments and research to further its understanding of extreme weather events so that it can make 
proactive changes.  The City's capital improvement planning process also considers the criticality 
of each facility to help prioritize resiliency improvements for facilities that are most critical to the 
continued operations of the Water Reclamation System.  
Cybersecurity 
Computer networks and systems used for data  transmission and collection are vital to the 
efficient operation of the Water Reclamation System.  Increasi ngly, governmental entities are 
being targeted by cyberattacks.  A rapidly ch anging cyber risk landscape may introduce new 
vulnerabilities that attackers/hackers can explo it in attempts to effect breaches or service 
disruptions.  The potential disruption, access, modification, disclosure, or destruction of data could 
result in the initiation of legal claims or proceed ings, liability under laws that protect the privacy 
of personal information, regulatory penalties, disruptions in operations and the services provided, 
and negatively impact Pledged Revenues. 
Legislation Impacting the Water Reclamation System 
From time to time, legislation is introduced in the Florida Legislature, which could impact 
the Water Reclamation System.  Such proposed legislation includes, but is not limited to, 
legislation affecting the financial relationship municipal utilities have with other jurisdictions and 
rates, fees, and charges that a mu nicipal utility could charge customers outside of its boundaries.  
There can be no assurance that such legislation or other legislation will not be introduced or 
enacted in the future th at would, or might appl y to, or have a material  adverse effect upon, the 
operations of the Water Reclamation System or the financial results of the Water Reclamation 
Fund. 
Enforceability of Remedies 
The remedies available to the owners of the Series 2024 Bonds upon an Event of Default 
under the Bond Ordinance are in ma ny respects dependent upon judicial actions which are often 
subject to discretion and delay.  Under existing constitutional and statutory law and judicial 
decisions, including specifically Title 11 of the United States Code, the remedies specified by the 
federal bankruptcy code, the Bond Ordinance, and the Series 2024 Bonds may not be readily 
available or may be limited.  The various legal opi nions to be delivered concurrently with the 
delivery of the Series 2024 Bonds (includi ng Co-Bond Counsel's approving opinion) will be 
qualified, as to the enforceability of the vari ous legal instruments, by limitations imposed by  
66 
(a) bankruptcy, reorganization, in solvency, or other s imilar laws affecting the enforcement of 
creditors' rights generally enacted before or after such delivery, (b) equitable principles, and (c) the 
exercise of judicial discretion.  See "A PPENDIX B – THE BOND ORDINANCE" attached 
hereto. 
LITIGATION 
The City, like other similar bodies, is subject to  a variety of suits and proceedings arising 
in the ordinary conduct of its affairs.  The Cit y, after reviewing the curre nt status of all pending 
and threatened litigation, believes that, while the outcome of litigation cannot be predicted, the 
final settlement of all lawsuits which have been filed and of any actions or claims pending or, to 
the knowledge of the City, threatened against the City or its officials in such capacity will not have 
a material adverse effect upon the financial pos ition or results of operations of the Water 
Reclamation System. 
There is no litigation now pending or, to the knowledge of the City, threatened against the 
City which restrains or enjoins the issuance or delivery of the Series 2024 Bonds or the use of the 
proceeds of the Series 2024 Bonds or which questions  or contests the validity of the Series 2024 
Bonds or the proceedings and authority under which they are to be issued, executed, and delivered.  
Neither the creation, organization, nor existence of the City, nor the title of the present members 
or other officials of the City to their respective offices, is being currently contested or questioned 
to the knowledge of the City. 
TAX MATTERS 
General 
The Code establishes certain requirements whic h must be met subsequent to the issuance 
of the Series 2024 Bonds in order that interest  on the Series 2024 Bonds be and remain excluded 
from gross income for purposes of federal income taxation.  Non-compliance may cause interest 
on the Series 2024 Bonds to be included in federal gross income retroactive to the date of issuance 
of the Series 2024 Bonds, regardless of the da te on which such non-compliance occurs or is 
ascertained.  These requirements include, but are not limited to, provisions which prescribe yield 
and other limits within which the proceeds of the Series 2024 Bonds and the other amounts are to 
be invested and require that certain investment  earnings on the foregoin g must be rebated on a 
periodic basis to the Treasury Department of th e United States.  The City has covenanted in the 
Series 2024 Resolution with respect to the Series  2024 Bonds to comply with such requirements 
in order to maintain  the exclusion from federa l gross income of  the interest on  the Series 2024 
Bonds. 
In the opinion of Co-Bond Counsel, assuming compliance with certain covenants, under 
existing laws, regulations, judicial decisions an d rulings, interest on the Series 2024 Bonds is 
excluded from gross income for purposes of federal income taxation.  Interest on the Series 2024 
Bonds is not an item of tax preference for purposes of the fe deral alternative minimum tax; 
however, interest on the Series 2024 Bonds may be included in the "adjusted financial statement  
67 
income" of certain "applicable corporations" that are subject to the 15-percent alternative 
minimum tax under section 55 of the Code. 
Except as described above, Co-Bond Counsel  will express no opinion regarding other 
federal income tax consequences resulting from the ownership of, receipt or accrual of interest on, 
or disposition of Series 2024 Bonds.  Prospective purchasers of Series 2024 Bonds should be aware 
that the ownership of Series 2024 Bonds may result in collateral federal income tax consequences, 
including (a) the denial of a deduction for interest on indebtedness incurre d or continued to 
purchase or carry Series 2024 Bonds; (b) the reduction of the lo ss reserve deduction for property 
and casualty insurance companies by fifteen percent (15%) of certain items, including interest on 
Series 2024 Bonds; (c) the inclusion of interest on Series 2024 Bonds in earnings of certain foreign 
corporations doing business in the United States for purposes of th e branch profits tax; (d) the 
inclusion of interest on Series 2024 Bonds in passive income subject to federal income taxation of 
certain Subchapter S corporations with Subchapter C earnings and profits at the close of the taxable 
year; and (d) the inclusion of in terest on Series 2024 Bonds in "modified adjusted gross income" 
by recipients of certain Social Security and Railroad Retiremen t benefits for the purposes of 
determining whether such benefits are included in gross income for federal income tax purposes. 
As to questions of fact material to the opinion of Co-Bond Counsel, Co-Bond Counsel will 
rely upon representations and covenants made on be half of the City, certif icates of appropriate 
officers and certificates of public officials (including certifications as to the use of proceeds of the 
Series 2024 Bonds and of the property financed or  refinanced thereby), without undertaking to 
verify the same by independent investigation. 
PURCHASE, OWNERSHIP, SALE OR DISPOSITION OF THE SERIES 2024 BONDS 
AND THE RECEIPT OR ACCRUAL OF THE INTEREST THEREON MAY HAVE ADVERSE 
FEDERAL TAX CONSEQUENCES FOR CERTAIN INDIVIDUAL AND CORPORATE 
BONDHOLDERS, INCLUDING , BUT NOT LIMITED TO, THE CONSEQUENCES 
DESCRIBED ABOVE.  PROSPECTIVE BO NDHOLDERS SHOULD CONSULT WITH 
THEIR TAX SPECIALISTS FOR INFORMATION IN THAT REGARD. 
Information Reporting and Backup Withholding 
Interest paid on tax-exempt bonds such as the Series 2024 Bonds is subject to information 
reporting to the Internal Revenue Service in a manner similar to interest paid on taxable 
obligations.  This reporting requirement does not affect the excludability of interest on the Series 
2024 Bonds from gross income for federal income tax purposes.  However, in conjunction with 
that information reporting requirement, the Code subjects certain non-corporate owners of Series 
2024 Bonds, under certain circumstances, to "backup withholding" at the rate specified in the Code 
with respect to payments on the Series 2024 B onds and proceeds from the sale of Series 2024 
Bonds.  Any amount so withheld would be refunde d or allowed as a credit against the federal 
income tax of such owner of Series 2024 Bonds.  This withholding generally applies if the owner 
of Series 2024 Bonds (a) fails to furnish the payor such owner's social security number or other 
taxpayer identification number ("TIN"), (b) furnished the payor an incorrect TIN, (c) fails to 
properly report interest, dividends, or other "reportable payments " as defined in the Code, or 
(d) under certain circumstances, fails to provide the payor or such owner's securities broker with a 
certified statement, signed under penalty of perjury, that the TIN provided is correct and that such  
68 
owner is not subject to backup withholding.  Prospective purchasers of the Series 2024 Bonds may 
also wish to consult with their tax advisors with respect to the need to furnish certain taxpayer 
information in order to avoid backup withholding. 
Other Tax Matters 
During recent years, legislative proposals have been introduced in Congress, and in some 
cases enacted, that altered certain federal tax consequences resulting from the ownership of 
obligations that are similar to  the Series 2024 Bonds.  In some cases, th ese proposals have 
contained provisions that altered these consequences on a retroactiv e basis.  Such alteration of 
federal tax consequences may have affected the ma rket value of obligations similar to the Series 
2024 Bonds.  From time to time, le gislative proposals are pending which could have an effect on 
both the federal tax consequences resulting fro m ownership of the Series 2024 Bonds and their 
market value.  No assurance can be given that legislative proposals will not be enacted that would 
apply to, or have an adverse effect upon, the Series 2024 Bonds.   
Prospective purchasers of the Series 2024 Bonds should consult their own tax advisors as 
to the tax consequences of owning the Series  2024 Bonds in their part icular state or local 
jurisdiction and regarding any pending or proposed federal or state tax legislation, regulations or 
litigation, as to which Bond Counsel expresses no opinion. 
Tax Treatment of Bond Premium 
The difference between the principal amount of the Series 2024 Bonds and the initial 
offering price to the pub lic (excluding bond houses, brokers or similar persons or organizations 
acting in the capacity of underwriters or wholesalers) at which price a substantial amount of such 
Series 2024 Bonds of the same maturity and, if applicable, interest rate, was sold constitutes to an 
initial purchaser amortizable bond premium which is not deductible from gross income for federal 
income tax purposes.  The amount of amortizable bond premium for a taxable year is determined 
actuarially on a constant interest rate basis over the term of each of the Series 2024 Bonds, which 
ends on the earlier of the maturity or call date for each of the Series 2024 Bonds which minimizes 
the yield on such Series 2024 Bonds to the purchaser.  For purposes of determining gain or loss on 
the sale or other disposition of a Series 2024 Bond, an initial purchas er who acquires such 
obligation in the initial offering is required to d ecrease such purchaser’s adjusted basis in such 
Series 2024 Bond annually by the amount of amortizable bond premium for the taxable year.  The 
amortization of bond premium may be taken into account as a reduction in the amount of tax-
exempt income for purposes of determining various other tax consequences of owning such Series 
2024 Bonds.  Bondholders of the Series 2024 Bonds are advised that they should consult with their 
own tax advisors with respect to the state and local tax consequences of owning such Series 2024 
Bonds.

RATINGS 
S&P Global Ratings and Fitch Ratings (togethe r, the "Rating Agencies") have assigned 
ratings of "AAA" and "AAA," respectively, to the Series 2024 Bonds. 
The ratings, including any related outlook with respect to potential changes in such ratings, 
reflect only the respective views of the Rating Agencies, and an explanation of the significance of 
such ratings may be obtained from the Rating Agencies furnishing the ratings.  Generally, a rating 
agency bases its rating on the in formation and materials furnishe d to it and on investigations, 
studies, and assumptions of its own.  There is no assurance that such ratings will remain unchanged 
for any given period of time or that they will not be revised downward or withdrawn entirely by 
the rating agency furnishing the sa me, if, in its judgment, circum stances so warrant.  Any such 
downward revision or withdrawal of such ratings or other actions by the Rating Agencies or either 
of them, may have an adverse effect on the liquid ity and/or market price of the affected Series 
2024 Bonds.  The City has not undertaken any responsibility to oppose any such revision, 
suspension or withdrawal.

1.0 Introduction 
The City of Orlando (City) authorized CDM Smith Inc. (CDM Smith) to prepare an Engineer’s Report in 
support of the City’s planned issuance of approximately $140 million of its Water Reclamation System 
Improvement Revenue Bonds, Series 2024A (Series 2024A Bonds) to, among other things, fund various 
wastewater and reclaimed water capital improvements projects and approximately $17 million of its 
Water Reclamation System Refunding Revenue Bonds, Series 2024B (Series 2024B Bonds) to refund and 
redeem the City's outstanding Wastewater System Refunding and Improvement Revenue Bonds, Series 
2013 (Series 2013 Bonds). CDM Smith was retained by the City to project annual revenue requirements 
for Fiscal Years 2024 through 2028, including operating expenses, existing and prospective debt service, 
administration fees, depreciation, renewal and replacement requirements and capital expenditures. 
This report presents the results of a revenue analysis and describes the organization and management 
of the City’s Public Works Department and specific responsibilities. The report outlines the City’s 
wastewater and reclaimed water service areas, facilities, operations, capital improvement projects for 
funding, and historical and projected financial performance of the Wastewater Utility under the 
wastewater fee structure that was adopted by the City to be effective October 1, 2024. 
The report findings and conclusions are presented in Section 7. In summary, this report demonstrates 
that the City’s currently implemented wastewater and reclaimed water rate structure is adequate to 
fund the utility’s operations, capital improvement program, proposed debt service and anticipated debt 
service coverage requirements through Fiscal Year 2028. Figure 1.1 illustrates the estimated annual debt 
service coverage for the for Fiscal Years 2022 through 2028 in support of the Series 2024A Bonds and 
the Series 2024B Bonds (together, Series 2024 Bonds) based upon the revenue analysis presented in the 
following sections of this report. 
1.1 Water Reclamation Division 
Figure 1.2 presents the organizational chart for the City’s Water Reclamation Division (WRD) under the 
Public Works Department. The Public Works Department is responsible for the operation and 
maintenance of the system facilities. Corey Knight, P.E. is the Director of Public Works. 
The WRD Manager, Paul Deuel oversees the operations, administration, and budgets, of all wastewater 
sections, and is supported by two Assistant Division Managers. Daron Johnson is the Assistant Division 
Manager for the Wastewater Process/Operations Section, consisting of the Conserv I, Conserv II, and 
Iron Bridge Water Reclamation Facilities, Industrial Automation Group (IAG), Environmental Laboratory 
Services, the Easterly Wetlands, and Compliance and Public Awareness; each of which are led by a 
Supervisor. Charles Shultz is the Assistant Division Manager for the Wastewater Engineering/ 
Maintenance Section. The Wastewater Engineering/Maintenance Section consists of System Evaluation 
and Maintenance, Lift Station Operations, and Wastewater Engineering and Mapping; each of which are 
led by a Supervisor.

2.0 Wastewater System Service Area and Facilities 
The City’s wastewater territorial area spans an area of approximately 110 square miles. The City’s 
wastewater territorial area is shown on Figure 2.1. With recent annexations, the City’s incorporated 
area has expanded to approximately 138 square miles, with areas outside the City’s wastewater 
territorial area being typically served by Orange County Utilities. The City’s wastewater customers 
include single-family residential, multi-family residential, commercial properties, industrial facilities, 
theme parks, government facilities, and schools. The City serves approximately 84,400 residential and 
commercial wastewater accounts. 
The City’s wastewater system is comprised of three water reclamation facilities, a collection system, and 
a transmission system with force mains and lift stations. The three water reclamation facilities (WRFs) 
are the Iron Bridge Regional WRF, Conserv I WRF, and Conserv II WRF. The Iron Bridge WRF receives 
wastewater from the City of Orlando, as well as other local government utilities including Winter Park, 
Maitland, Casselberry, and portions of Orange and Seminole Counties. Each WRF is described and 
discussed in greater detail in Section 4, Wastewater Facilities Visual Inspection and Review. 
2.1 Wastewater Transmission System 
The City conveys wastewater to the three water reclamation facilities through its collection and 
transmission system. There are approximately 15,625 manholes, 232 lift stations and over 852 miles of 
piping responsible for collecting and transmitting the wastewater for treatment. The system has the 
ability to transfer wastewater from the eastern part of the City to the western part of the City allowing 
the flexibility to equalize flows between the WRFs. This ability also allows the system operators to shift 
the quantity of reclaimed water available to the east and west portions of the City. 
2.2 Historical Flows and Capacity 
The City’s wastewater flows are directly influenced by growth. The wastewater flows, in million gallons 
per day (MGD), feeding into each facility had a steady growth from the year 2012 through 2019, the 
data shows a decline in flow from 2019 through 2021, most likely due to COVID, followed with a distinct 
increase in years 2022 and 2023. The flows are listed in Table 2.1 and displayed graphically on Figure 
2.2. 
Table 2.1 Historical Influent Flows (MGD) for the City’s Water Reclamation Facilities (WRFs)

3.0 Reclaimed Water System 
Reclaimed water is domestic wastewater which has been treated, filtered and disinfected to a high 
degree such that it can be safely used to irrigate golf courses, parks, residential lawns and other public 
access areas. The City produces reclaimed water at the City’s three water reclamation facilities (WRFs): 
Conserv I, Conserv II, and Iron Bridge. From the WRFs, the reclaimed water is pumped through a 
network of pipes to end-users. The City’s reclaimed water service area and distribution piping is shown 
on Figure 3.1. 
The City’s reclaimed water customers include single-family homes, multi-family homes, commercial 
properties, industrial facilities, golf courses, theme parks, schools, and hotels. There are approximately 
7,407 residential and commercial accounts. The majority of the usage is for golf courses and single-
family residential neighborhoods in Baldwin Park and Lake Nona. The Orlando International Airport 
(OIA) is also a large user of reclaimed water for irrigating landscaping. 
3.1 Transmission and Distribution System 
The reclaimed water distribution systems are separated into the eastern and western areas. The 
western reclaimed water distribution system is fed solely from the Conserv II WRF and delivers water to 
local reuse and the Water Conserv II distribution center in Winter Garden, Florida. The Iron Bridge and 
Conserv I WRFs feed the Eastern Regional Reclaimed Water Distribution System (ERRWDS). The ERRWDS 
delivers water to City’s reclaimed water customers as well as multiple other reclaimed water utility 
providers. Combined, the two systems comprise approximately 215 miles of reclaimed water pipe 
ranging in size from 1-inch – 48-inches in diameter. 
3.2 Historical Flows 
The City’s reclaimed water system has continued to grow since its inception. The City enacted an 
ordinance in 2002 which requires reclaimed water piping to be installed in new residential and 
commercial developments if the development falls within a designated reclaimed water service area. 
The same ordinance requires customer connection to the reclaimed water system for irrigation when 
reclaimed water is made available to the property. The impact of these efforts will increase reclaimed 
water use and reduce the amount of potable water used for irrigation as shown on Figure 3.2 and 
reflected numerically in Table 3.1.  
Table 3.1 City of Orlando Water Reclamation Facility Historical Reclaimed Water Flows (MGD) 
WRF Year 
2020 2021 2022 2023 
Conserv I 5.35 4.92 5.25 5.46 
Conserv II 14.33 14.02 16.64 15.99 
Iron Bridge 5.06 5.94 4.68 4.00 
Total 24.74 24.88 26.57 25.45

4.0 Wastewater Facilities Visual Inspection and 
Review 
To review the current operation and condition of the wastewater facilities, visual inspections and staff 
interviews were conducted for the three water reclamation facilities, as well as a representative sample 
of lift stations. In addition to these facility inspections, the following documents were reviewed to 
obtain supplemental information regarding the facilities and regulatory compliance: 
▬ Iron Bridge Regional WRF Capacity Analysis Report, dated December 10, 2019 
▬ Iron Bridge Regional WRF Operation & Maintenance Performance Report, December 10, 2019 
▬ Water Conserv I WRF Capacity Analysis Report, dated September 15, 2020 
▬ Water Conserv I WRF Operation & Maintenance Performance Report, dated September 20, 
2020 
▬ Water Conserv II WRF Capacity Analysis Report, dated May 15, 2021 
▬ Water Conserv II WRF Operation & Maintenance Performance Report, dated May 15, 2021 
4.1 Visual Inspection 
Inspections and staff interviews were conducted for selected City of Orlando wastewater system 
facilities. Findings are summarized in the following subsections. 
4.1.1 Iron Bridge Regional WRF 
The Iron Bridge Regional WRF (IBRWRF) has a permitted annual average daily flow (AADF) capacity of 40 
mgd and has a current AADF of 25.2 mgd. A site visit was conducted to visually inspect the facility. The 
results of the inspection, which included the following unit processes, are summarized in Table 4.1 and 
selected features are documented in Pictures 4.1 through 4.4. 
▬ Master Pumping Station and Influent Screening 
▬ Pretreatment (Grit Removal) 
▬ Flow Equalization 
▬ Biological Nutrient Removal 
▬ Secondary Clarification 
▬ Tertiary Filtration 
▬ Disinfection and Dechlorination 
▬ Post Aeration 
▬ Effluent (Reclaimed Water) Handling 
▬ Biosolids Handling, (Storage, Thickening, Dewatering and Lime Stabilization)

4.2 Regulatory Compliance Requirements 
To assess regulatory compliance, Florida Department of Environmental Protection (FDEP) Monthly 
Discharge Monitoring Reports (DMRs) from 2019 to 2023 were reviewed, as well as compliance reports 
and correspondence between the City and FDEP. Based on a review of this information, the WRFs are 
consistently within permit limits, with only a few permit compliance items identified in 2023, such as: 
▬ Elevated TSS and nutrient concentrations attributable to higher-than-normal flows and possible 
wildlife in the area. Corrective measures/actions were established to prevent this from occurring 
in the future. 
▬ Elevated nitrogen and phosphorus levels due to equipment failure and issues with a repair 
vendor. The City Purchasing Department worked to find new vendors to repair the damaged 
equipment. 
▬ An instance where a toxic load entered the plant resulting in elevated TSS and Fecal Coliform 
levels. Effluent was being discharged to the RIBs when this spike occurred. 
A-214.0 │ WASTEWATER FACILITIES VISUAL INSPECTION AND REVIEW 
WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEMS REVENUE BONDS, SERIES 2024 A AND 2024B │ PAGE 4-9 
▬  Compliance monitoring well and background monitoring well pH exceedances on multiple 
instances due to pre-existing background conditions, two turbidity exceedances likely due to 
sampling error and a TDS excursion that has previously been documented with FDEP. 
The City has received warning letters and was put under consent order by FDEP for numerous 
unauthorized discharges. However, the City is working with the FDEP and making improvements 
necessary to attempt to eliminate unauthorized discharges. It should also be noted that the warning 
letters received in February of 2023 were for unauthorized discharges that occurred during Hurricane 
Ian. 
From a review of the DMRs, the WRFs are operating near 70 percent of permitted capacity (See Figure 
4.1) and well within permit limits (See Table 4.5). 
 
Figure 4.1 Water Reclamation Facilities – Percent of Permitted Now   
EPA has recently promulgated regulations regarding the allowable levels of PFAS in drinking water. 
While limits have not yet been established for wastewater, the City anticipates that rules will be 
forthcoming in the future. As PFAS in wastewater tends to concentrate in the biosolids, the City has 
taken the initiative in evaluating technology that may be capable of destroying PFAS contained therein. 
Working in cooperation with the Florida Department of Environmental Protection (FDEP), the city 
received a Bipartisan Infrastructure Law grant to host a pilot project testing PFAS destruction technology 
at the city's Iron Bridge plant. The pilot unit, which uses supercritical water oxidation, has been 
A-224.0 │ WASTEWATER FACILITIES VISUAL INSPECTION AND REVIEW 
WATER RECLAMATION SYSTEMS REVENUE BONDS, SERIES 2024 A AND 2024B │ PAGE 4-10 
delivered to Iron Bridge and is expected to be operational In October 2024. The pilot test will run for a 
period of three months and the results will be shared with FDEP and the industry as a whole.
